[Sources](#sources)\n    27→\n    28→---\n    29→\n    30→## Overview\n    31→\n    32→Observability is the ability to understand a system's internal state from its external outputs.\n    33→For performance engineering, observability answers: \"Why is my system slow, and where?\"\n    34→\n    35→**Key industry numbers:**\n    36→\n    37→- The global observability market reached $28.5 billion in 2025 (Gartner/Research Nester).\n    38→- 15-25% of infrastructure budgets are allocated to observability (Gartner).\n    39→- Over 50% of observability spend goes to logs alone (ClickHouse TCO Report).\n    40→- 97% of organizations have experienced unexpected observability cost surprises (Grepr AI, 2026).\n    41→- 36% of enterprise clients spend over $1M/year on observability; 4% exceed $10M (Gartner).\n    42→\n    43→Observability is not monitoring. Monitoring tells you *what* is broken. Observability tells you *why*.\n    44→\n    45→---\n    46→\n    47→## The Three Pillars: Metrics, Logs, Traces\n    48→\n    49→### Metrics\n    50→\n    51→Numeric measurements of system behavior over time. Stored as time series.\n    52→\n    53→| Property         | Detail                                                    |\n    54→|------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------|\n    55→| **Data type**    | Counters, gauges, histograms, summaries                   |\n    56→| **Storage cost** | Low (~8 bytes per data point in Prometheus TSDB)          |\n    57→| **Query speed**  | Fast (pre-aggregated, indexed by label)                   |\n    58→| **Best for**     | Dashboards, alerting, trend analysis, capacity planning   |\n    59→| **Cardinality**  | Must be bounded (unbounded labels destroy performance)    |\n    60→\n    61→Typical performance metrics: request rate, error rate, p50/p95/p99 latency, CPU utilization,\n    62→memory usage, queue depth, connection pool saturation.\n    63→\n    64→### Logs\n    65→\n    66→Discrete events with structured or unstructured text.\n    67→\n    68→| Property         | Detail                                                    |\n    69→|------------------|-----------------------------------------------------------|\n    70→| **Data type**    | Text records with timestamps and metadata                 |\n    71→| **Storage cost** | High (can be 10-100x more than metrics at scale)          |\n    72→| **Query speed**  | Slow without indexing; fast with structured/indexed logs   |\n    73→| **Best for**     | Debugging, audit trails, error details, forensic analysis |\n    74→| **Volume risk**  | Easily grows to TB/day in production systems              |\n    75→\n    76→Performance-relevant log patterns: slow query logs (>100ms), garbage collection pauses,\n    77→connection timeouts, circuit breaker state changes, retry exhaustion events.\n    78→\n    79→### Traces\n    80→"}]},"uuid":"affc1256-56c2-48e5-a7a5-379a7642c1d6","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:36:21.772Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"077602fe-5cd0-4ebb-9513-8fa358f866cb","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

{"parentUuid":"0455d780-e7a1-4460-a1a2-86a37fd21792","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"67fcde4a-7483-4526-a2da-9ae9514890c8","agentId":"a722af01c5594aba0","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_017MfoY6dbHVqf2K5RNLCn7v","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Event-Driven Architecture — Architecture Expertise Module\n     2→\n     3→> Event-Driven Architecture (EDA) is a design paradigm where services communicate through events rather than direct calls. Producers emit events without knowing who consumes them; consumers react independently. This achieves loose coupling and high scalability at the cost of debugging complexity and eventual consistency.\n     4→\n     5→> **Category:** Pattern\n     6→> **Complexity:** Complex\n     7→> **Applies when:** Systems requiring loose coupling, high-throughput event processing, audit trails, or complex workflows spanning multiple services\n     8→\n     9→---\n    10→\n    11→## What This Is (and What It Isn't)\n    12→\n    13→### Events vs Commands vs Messages\n    14→\n    15→These three terms are conflated constantly. They are not the same thing.\n    16→\n    17→An **event** is a notification that something has already happened. It is past tense, immutable, and carries no expectation of a response. `OrderPlaced`, `PaymentCompleted`, `UserRegistered`. The producer does not know — and must not care — who receives it. Events are facts. They cannot be rejected by consumers.\n    18→\n    19→A **command** is a request for something to happen. It is imperative, directed at a specific handler, and expects exactly one consumer. `PlaceOrder`, `ChargePayment`, `SendEmail`. Commands can fail. They carry intent and imply a contract between sender and receiver.\n    20→\n    21→A **message** is the envelope. Both events and commands travel as messages through brokers, queues, or buses. \"Message-driven\" describes the transport mechanism. \"Event-driven\" describes the semantic pattern. A system can be message-driven without being event-driven (e.g., command queues), and theoretically event-driven without messages (e.g., polling a change log).\n    22→\n    23→**The litmus test:** If removing all consumers would break the producer, you have commands disguised as events. True events are fire-and-forget from the producer's perspective.\n    24→\n    25→### EDA Is Not Event Sourcing\n    26→\n    27→Event-Driven Architecture and Event Sourcing are orthogonal concepts that happen to share the word \"event.\"\n    28→\n    29→**EDA** is a communication pattern between services. Service A emits an event; Services B, C, D react to it. The events are transient signals — once consumed, they have served their purpose (though they may be retained in a log for replay).\n    30→\n    31→**Event Sourcing** is a persistence pattern within a single service. Instead of storing current state, you store the sequence of events that produced that state. The event log is the system of record. Current state is derived by replaying events.\n    32→\n    33→You can use EDA without Event Sourcing (most systems do). You can use Event Sourcing without EDA (a single monolith with an event-sourced aggregate). You can use both together (a CQRS/ES service that also publishes domain events to a broker). Conflating them leads to architectures that are complex for no reason.\n    34→\n    35→### Choreography vs Orchestration\n    36→\n    37→These are the two fundamental coordination models in event-driven systems.\n    38→\n    39→**Choreography** is decentralized. Each service listens for events and decides independently what to do. There is no central coordinator. An `OrderPlaced` event triggers the inventory service to reserve stock, the payment service to charge the card, the notification service to send a confirmation — all independently. The \"workflow\" emerges from the collective behavior of autonomous services reacting to events.\n    40→\n    41→- **Strengths:** Loose coupling, independent deployability, no single point of failure, scales well when workflows are simple.\n    42→- **Weaknesses:** The workflow is implicit and scattered across codebases. Nobody owns the end-to-end flow. Debugging \"why did this order fail?\" requires correlating logs across every service. Adding a new step means modifying a consumer, and the existing producers are unaware. Circular event chains can create infinite loops.\n    43→\n    44→**Orchestration** is centralized. A coordinator service (the orchestrator) owns the workflow definition and issues commands to each participant. The orchestrator knows the sequence: first reserve inventory, then charge payment, then send notification. Participants respond with results, and the orchestrator decides the next step.\n    45→\n    46→- **Strengths:** The workflow is explicit, visible, and debuggable. Compensation logic (rollbacks) lives in one place. Adding or reordering steps is straightforward.\n    47→- **Weaknesses:** The orchestrator is a coupling point and potential bottleneck. It must be highly available. It knows about every participant, creating a dependency fan-out.\n    48→\n    49→**The pragmatic answer:** Use choreography for simple, loosely related reactions (notifications, analytics, audit logging). Use orchestration for multi-step business-critical workflows where you need visibility and compensation (order fulfillment, payment processing, onboarding flows). Most production systems use both — choreography for fire-and-forget side effects, orchestration for the critical path. Tools like Temporal, Step Functions, and Conductor provide orchestration frameworks purpose-built for this.\n    50→\n    51→### The Four EDA Patterns\n    52→\n    53→Martin Fowler identifies four distinct patterns that all fall under \"event-driven\":\n    54→\n    55→1. **Event Notification** — The simplest form. A service emits an event saying \"something happened\" with minimal data (just an ID and event type). Consumers must call back to the source to get details. Low coupling but high chattiness.\n    56→\n    57→2. **Event-Carried State Transfer (ECST)** — Events carry the full relevant state. `OrderPlaced { orderId, items, shippingAddress, total }`. Consumers have everything they need without callbacks. Reduces coupling further but increases event size and raises staleness risks.\n    58→\n    59→3. **Event Sourcing** — Events are the system of record. State is derived from the event log. Covered in detail in the CQRS/Event Sourcing module.\n    60→\n    61→4. **CQRS** — Command Query Responsibility Segregation. Read and write models are separated, often connected by events. The write side emits events; the read side builds projections. Covered in the CQRS/Event Sourcing module.\n    62→\n    63→### \"Just Use Kafka\" Is Not EDA\n    64→\n    65→Kafka is an infrastructure component, not an architecture. Installing Kafka and publishing JSON blobs to topics does not make your system event-driven. Common anti-patterns:\n    66→\n    67→- **Request-response over Kafka:** Producing a command to a topic and polling a response topic. You have reinvented HTTP with worse latency and no type safety.\n    68→- **Database replication via events:** Mirroring every database row change as an event. You have built a slow, unreliable database replica. Use CDC tools (Debezium) if you actually need this, and do not pretend it is EDA.\n    69→- **Kafka as a database:** Storing state in Kafka topics and reading it back. Kafka is an append-only log, not a query engine. Use it as a transport or audit log, not a primary datastore (unless you are building a very specific streaming application with KTables).\n    70→\n    71→---\n    72→\n    73→## When to Use It\n    74→\n    75→### Decoupling Services That Evolve Independently\n    76→\n    77→When teams own different services and deploy on different schedules, synchronous coupling creates coordination nightmares. EDA allows services to communicate through contracts (event schemas) without runtime dependencies. The payment service does not import the order service's SDK — it subscribes to `OrderPlaced` events.\n    78→\n    79→**Real example — Shopify:** Shopify processes approximately 66 million messages per second at peak through its Kafka backbone. Each domain (inventory, payments, search, analytics) consumes events independently. When the search team wants to re-index products, they replay events from the product catalog topic without coordinating with the catalog team.\n    80→"}]},"uuid":"56997958-f9cf-418d-9d90-b6effc7276fe","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:36:22.358Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0455d780-e7a1-4460-a1a2-86a37fd21792","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

raw test runner stdout+stderr\n     9→ * @returns {{ total: number, passed: number, failed: number, skipped: number }}\n    10→ */\n    11→export function parseTestOutput(output) {\n    12→  const result = { total: 0, passed: 0, failed: 0, skipped: 0 };\n    13→\n    14→  if (!output) {\n    15→    return result;\n    16→  }\n    17→\n    18→  // Node built-in test runner summary lines:\n    19→  //   # tests 12\n    20→  //   # pass 10\n    21→  //   # fail 1\n    22→  //   # skipped 1\n    23→  const totalMatch = output.match(/^# tests\\s+(\\d+)/m);\n    24→  const passMatch = output.match(/^# pass\\s+(\\d+)/m);\n    25→  const failMatch = output.match(/^# fail\\s+(\\d+)/m);\n    26→  const skipMatch = output.match(/^# skipped\\s+(\\d+)/m);\n    27→\n    28→  if (totalMatch) {\n    29→    result.total = Number.parseInt(totalMatch[1], 10);\n    30→  }\n    31→\n    32→  if (passMatch) {\n    33→    result.passed = Number.parseInt(passMatch[1], 10);\n    34→  }\n    35→\n    36→  if (failMatch) {\n    37→    result.failed = Number.parseInt(failMatch[1], 10);\n    38→  }\n    39→\n    40→  if (skipMatch) {\n    41→    result.skipped = Number.parseInt(skipMatch[1], 10);\n    42→  }\n    43→\n    44→  // Fallback: if no summary lines found, try TAP-style counting\n    45→  if (!totalMatch && !passMatch && !failMatch) {\n    46→    const okLines = output.match(/^ok \\d+/gm);\n    47→    const notOkLines = output.match(/^not ok \\d+/gm);\n    48→    const skipLines = output.match(/^ok \\d+ .+# skip/gim);\n    49→\n    50→    result.passed = (okLines?.length ?? 0) - (skipLines?.length ?? 0);\n    51→    result.failed = notOkLines?.length ?? 0;\n    52→    result.skipped = skipLines?.length ?? 0;\n    53→    result.total = result.passed + result.failed + result.skipped;\n    54→  }\n    55→\n    56→  return result;\n    57→}\n    58→\n    59→/**\n    60→ * Run tests and parse results, or return null on failure.\n    61→ * @param {string} projectRoot\n    62→ * @returns {{ total: number, passed: number, failed: number, skipped: number } | null}\n    63→ */\n    64→function collectTestResults(projectRoot) {\n    65→  try {\n    66→    const output = execFileSync(\n    67→      'node',\n    68→      ['--test', '--experimental-test-snapshots'],\n    69→      { cwd: projectRoot, encoding: 'utf8', timeout: 120_000 },\n    70→    );\n    71→\n    72→    return parseTestOutput(output);\n    73→  } catch (error) {\n    74→    // Test command may exit non-zero if tests fail — still parse output\n    75→    if (error.stdout || error.stderr) {\n    76→      return parseTestOutput(`${error.stdout ?? Number.parseInt(summaryMatch[3], 10) : 0;\n   104→  }\n   105→\n   106→  return result;\n   107→}\n   108→\n   109→/**\n   110→ * Parse `git diff --name-status` output into added/modified/deleted case 'D':\n   143→        result.deleted.push(filePath);\n   144→        break;\n   145→      default:\n   146→        // R (rename), C (copy), etc. — treat as modified\n   147→ @param {string} phase\n   229→ * @returns {number | null} duration in seconds, or null if unavailable\n   230→ */\n   231→function collectDuration(stateRoot, runId, phase) {\n   232→  const eventsPath = path.join(stateRoot, 'runs', runId, 'events.ndjson');\n   233→\n   234→  if (!fs.existsSync(eventsPath)) {\n   235→    return null;\n   236→  }\n   237→\n   238→  try {\n   239→    const content = fs.readFileSync(eventsPath, 'utf8');\n   240→    const lines = content.split('\\n').filter(Boolean);\n   241→\n   242→    let enterTime = null;\n   243→    let exitTime = null;\n   244→\n   245→    for (const line of lines) {\n   246→      const event = JSON.parse(line);\n   247→\n   248→      if (event.phase !== phase) {\n   249→        continue;\n   250→      }\n   251→\n   252→      if (event.event === 'phase_enter' && event.created_at) {\n   253→        enterTime = new Date(event.created_at).getTime();\n   254→      }\n   255→\n   256→      if (event.event === 'phase_exit' && event.created_at) {\n   257→        exitTime = new Date(event.created_at).getTime();\n   258→      }\n   259→    }\n   260→\n   261→    if (enterTime !== null && exitTime !== null && exitTime > enterTime) {\n   262→      return Math.round((exitTime - enterTime) / 1000);\n   263→    }\n   264→\n   265→    return null;\n   266→  } catch {\n   267→    return null;\n   268→  }\n   269→}\n   270→\n   271→/**\n   272→ * Collect metrics for a phase report.\n   273→ * @param {object} opts\n   274→ * @param {string} opts.projectRoot - project root path\n   275→ * @param {string} opts.stateRoot - state root path\n   276→ * @param {string} opts.runId - current run ID\n   277→ * @param {string} opts.phase - phase name (init|clarifier|executor|final_review)\n   278→ * @param {string} [opts.baseBranch] - base branch for diff (default: 'main')\n   279→ * @returns {object} structured metrics\n   280→ */\n   281→export function collectPhaseMetrics(opts) {\n   282→  const { projectRoot, stateRoot, runId, phase } = opts;\n   283→  const baseBranch = opts.baseBranch ?? 'main';\n   284→\n   285→  return {\n   286→    run_id: runId,\n   287→    phase,\n   288→    generated_at: new Date().toISOString(),\n   289→    tests: collectTestResults(projectRoot),\n   290→    diff: collectDiffStats(projectRoot, baseBranch),\n   291→    files: collectFileChanges(projectRoot, baseBranch),\n   292→    artifacts: collectArtifacts(stateRoot, runId),\n   293→    duration_seconds: collectDuration(stateRoot, runId, phase),\n   294→  };\n   295→}\n   296→\n   297→/**\n   298→ * Build a complete phase report JSON.\n   299→ * Deterministic fields from code, qualitative fields left as placeholders for agent.\n   300→ * @param {object} metrics - output from collectPhaseMetrics\n   301→ * @param {object} [qualitative] - agent-provided qualitative fields\n   302→ * @returns {object} complete report\n   303→ */\n   304→export function buildPhaseReport(metrics, qualitative = {}) {\n   305→  return {\n   306→    run_id: metrics.run_id,\n   307→    phase: metrics.phase,\n   308→    generated_at: metrics.generated_at,\n   309→    metrics: {\n   310→      tests: metrics.tests ?? { total: 0, passed: 0, failed: 0, skipped: 0 },\n   311→      diff: metrics.diff ?? { files_changed: 0, insertions: 0, deletions: 0 },\n   312→      files: metrics.files ?? { added: [], modified: [], deleted: [] },\n   313→      artifacts: metrics.artifacts ?? [],\n   314→      duration_seconds: metrics.duration_seconds ?? null,\n   315→    },\n   316→    qualitative: {\n   317→      summary: qualitative.summary ?? '',\n   318→      drift_analysis: qualitative.drift_analysis ?? '',\n   319→      decisions: qualitative.decisions ?? [],\n   320→      risks: qualitative.risks ?? [],\n   321→    },\n   322→  };\n   323→}\n   324→"}]},"uuid":"01209891-ce0a-4cb9-8181-f99b535d3dcc","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:36:22.690Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c08240fc-6067-4b56-bbfe-24b2c3f261ce","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

{"parentUuid":"d6e56f94-ce9a-440d-89fe-0e53ac9432dd","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a722af01c5594aba0","type":"progress","data":{"type":"hook_progress","hookEvent":"PostToolUse","hookName":"PostToolUse:Read","command":"callback"},"parentToolUseID":"toolu_01V6FWApXJ1T9Ndn3B9GhfQw","toolUseID":"toolu_01V6FWApXJ1T9Ndn3B9GhfQw","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:36:49.224Z","uuid":"8f9bd933-8d4d-4cb2-9b18-f2fbd3409ad1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}
{"parentUuid":"672c10b9-f783-4fe4-8576-5b3edcc4f125","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"67fcde4a-7483-4526-a2da-9ae9514890c8","agentId":"a722af01c5594aba0","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01V6FWApXJ1T9Ndn3B9GhfQw","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# Architecture Decision Records — Architecture Expertise Module\n     2→\n     3→> Architecture Decision Records (ADRs) document significant architectural decisions with context, consequences, and status. They answer \"why did we choose X?\" months or years later. Without ADRs, teams redebate the same decisions, new team members don't understand constraints, and architectural drift goes unnoticed.\n     4→\n     5→> **Category:** Decision\n     6→> **Complexity:** Simple\n     7→> **Applies when:** Any project making architectural decisions that will be questioned later — which is every project lasting more than 3 months\n     8→\n     9→**Cross-references:** [architectural-thinking], [technology-selection], [build-vs-buy], [domain-driven-design]\n    10→\n    11→---\n    12→\n    13→## What This Is (and What It Isn't)\n    14→\n    15→An Architecture Decision Record is a short document — typically one to two pages — that captures a single architectural decision: the context that made the decision necessary, the options that were considered, the decision that was made, and the consequences (both positive and negative) that are expected or observed. The format was introduced by Michael Nygard in his 2011 blog post \"Documenting Architecture Decisions\" at Cognitect, and has since become the de facto standard for lightweight architectural decision documentation across the industry. AWS, Google Cloud, Microsoft Azure, and the UK Government Digital Service (GDS) all formally recommend ADRs in their architecture guidance.\n    16→\n    17→Nygard's original format is deliberately minimal. It has exactly five sections:\n    18→\n    19→1. **Title** — A short noun phrase. \"ADR-007: Use PostgreSQL for the Orders Service,\" not \"We should probably think about databases.\"\n    20→2. **Status** — One of: Proposed, Accepted, Deprecated, Superseded. The status changes over time; the record itself does not. When a decision is reversed, the original ADR's status is updated to \"Superseded by ADR-NNN\" and a new ADR documents the reversal.\n    21→3. **Context** — The forces at play. What problem are we facing? What constraints exist? What requirements drove this decision? This section is the most important and most often under-written. Context without specifics — \"we need a scalable solution\" — is useless. Context with specifics — \"our projected write volume is 50k orders/day with 3x seasonal spikes, we have two engineers with PostgreSQL production experience and zero with MongoDB, and our query patterns require joins across 4 tables\" — is the information that makes the decision intelligible six months later.\n    22→4. **Decision** — The decision itself, stated in active voice. \"We will use PostgreSQL 16 with pgBouncer connection pooling.\" Not \"It was decided that...\" — passive voice obscures accountability.\n    23→5. **Consequences** — What happens because of this decision, both positive and negative. Good ADRs are honest about the negative consequences. \"We accept that horizontal write scaling will require a sharding strategy if volume exceeds 50k writes/minute\" is a consequence worth recording because it tells a future team member exactly when to revisit this decision.\n    24→\n    25→This is what an ADR **is**: a decision record. It captures the reasoning at a specific point in time, under specific constraints, with specific information available.\n    26→\n    27→This is what an ADR **is not**:\n    28→\n    29→**ADRs are not architecture documentation.** They do not describe the system's structure, component interactions, or deployment topology. That is the job of C4 diagrams, arc42 templates, or whatever structural documentation the team maintains. An ADR answers \"why did we choose X?\" — it does not answer \"how does the system work?\" Conflating the two produces documents that are too long, too broad, and serve neither purpose well. Olaf Zimmermann, professor at OST and a leading voice on ADR methodology, calls the conflation anti-pattern \"Blueprint in Disguise\" — an ADR that reads like a design document because the author stuffed component responsibilities, collaboration diagrams, and implementation details into what should have been a focused decision record.\n    30→\n    31→**ADRs are not requirements documents.** They do not specify what the system must do. They document a response to requirements and constraints that already exist. If the context section of your ADR reads like a requirements specification, the ADR is doing too much.\n    32→\n    33→**ADRs are not meeting minutes.** They capture the outcome and rationale of a decision, not the discussion that led to it. The meeting discussion is ephemeral; the decision and its reasoning are durable. Teams that try to capture the full discussion in the ADR produce documents that nobody reads because they are 12 pages long and buried in conversational tangents.\n    34→\n    35→**ADRs are not approval gates.** Some organizations turn ADRs into bureaucratic checkpoints where an Architecture Review Board must approve every decision before work can proceed. This is a misuse of the format. Nygard designed ADRs to be lightweight, written by the people closest to the decision, and reviewed by peers — not escalated through a hierarchy. When ADRs become approval gates, teams stop writing them because the overhead exceeds the value, and the decision history becomes invisible again.\n    36→\n    37→The power of the ADR format is its constraint. Five sections. One decision per record. Short enough that a new team member can read the entire ADR log in an afternoon and understand why the system has the shape it does. The moment an ADR format grows beyond these bounds — adding sections for \"stakeholder sign-off,\" \"risk assessment matrices,\" \"implementation timelines,\" and \"rollback plans\" — the format has been corrupted by process and will be abandoned by practitioners.\n    38→\n    39→---\n    40→\n    41→## When to Use It\n    42→\n    43→ADRs are warranted whenever a decision meets the \"would I explain this to a new team member?\" test. If a new engineer joins the team in six months and would need to understand why this particular choice was made in order to work effectively with the system, that decision deserves an ADR. The test is practical, not theoretical: would the absence of this record cause confusion, wasted investigation, or a re-debate of a settled question?\n    44→\n    45→**Any team larger than one person.** The moment a second person joins a project, the reasoning behind decisions can no longer live exclusively in one person's head. Even in a two-person team, decisions made by one engineer while the other was on vacation will be questioned when the second engineer encounters a surprising structural choice in the codebase. The ADR eliminates the \"why did you do it this way?\" conversation by providing the answer before the question is asked.\n    46→\n    47→**Decisions that are hard to reverse.** Jeff Bezos's one-way door / two-way door framework applies directly. One-way doors — choices that are expensive or impossible to undo once made — deserve the 15 to 30 minutes it takes to write an ADR. The storage engine for a service, the inter-service communication protocol, the authentication mechanism, the data consistency model, the choice to adopt or reject a framework that will be woven through the entire codebase — these are one-way doors. Recording the reasoning is insurance against the future moment when someone asks \"why didn't we use X instead?\" and no one who was present for the original decision is still on the team.\n    48→\n    49→**Technology choices.** Choosing between PostgreSQL and MongoDB, between REST and gRPC, between React and Vue, between AWS Lambda and ECS — each of these decisions has consequences that cascade through the system for years. An ADR captures not just what was chosen but what was rejected and why. This is critical: the rejected alternatives are often more informative than the chosen one, because they explain the constraints that shaped the decision.\n    50→\n    51→**Pattern choices.** Adopting event sourcing, choosing CQRS, selecting a modular monolith over microservices, implementing the strangler fig pattern for a legacy migration — these are structural decisions that define how the system works at a fundamental level. Without an ADR, a new team member encountering event sourcing in the codebase has no way to know whether it was a deliberate, considered choice or an experiment that was never cleaned up.\n    52→\n    53→**Organizational and process decisions with architectural impact.** The decision to adopt trunk-based development, to require feature flags for all new functionality, to use contract testing between services, or to adopt a specific branching strategy for database migrations — these are not code decisions, but they shape the architecture as surely as a technology choice does.\n    54→\n    55→**Cross-team decisions.** Any decision that affects more than one team — a shared API contract, a common authentication service, a standardized event schema — requires an ADR because the reasoning must be accessible to all affected teams, not just the one that made the decision.\n    56→\n    57→**Compliance and regulatory contexts.** In regulated industries (finance, healthcare, government), ADRs serve as an audit trail showing that architectural decisions were made deliberately, with consideration of alternatives and consequences. The UK Government Digital Service mandates ADRs for all technology decisions in government projects, and Google Cloud's Architecture Center recommends them as part of their Well-Architected Framework.\n    58→\n    59→---\n    60→\n    61→## When NOT to Use It\n    62→\n    63→The failure modes of over-applying ADRs are as real and as damaging as the failure modes of not having them at all. An organization drowning in unread ADRs is not better off than one with no ADRs — it is worse off, because team members develop the habit of ignoring architectural documentation entirely, and that habit persists even when a genuinely important ADR is written.\n    64→\n    65→**Trivial decisions that are easily reversible.** Choosing a logging library, selecting a date formatting utility, picking a folder structure for test files, naming a REST endpoint — these are two-way doors. They can be changed in hours or days with minimal impact. Writing an ADR for every minor library choice buries the important decisions in a sea of noise. If your ADR log has 200 entries and 180 of them document trivial choices, the 20 that matter are invisible. The signal-to-noise ratio of the ADR log is its most important property.\n    66→\n    67→**When ADRs become bureaucratic overhead.** If writing an ADR requires scheduling a meeting with an Architecture Review Board, filling out a 15-field template, getting three levels of sign-off, and waiting two weeks for approval before any work can proceed, the ADR process has been weaponized against the people it was meant to help. Teams in this situation stop writing ADRs — or worse, they write performative ADRs that satisfy the process without capturing real reasoning. The ADR becomes a tax on productivity rather than an investment in clarity. Nygard designed ADRs to be written by the decision-maker in 15 to 30 minutes. Any process that inflates that to days or weeks has broken the format.\n    68→\n    69→**When nobody reads them.** An ADR that is written and never read is waste. If the team's ADR log exists in a Confluence space that no one visits, or in a docs/ folder that is not part of the onboarding process, or in a GitHub wiki that is not linked from the README, the effort of writing ADRs is producing no value. Before adding more ADRs, fix the discoverability problem. Put the ADR log where people actually look: in the repository alongside the code, linked from the README, referenced in code comments at the point where the decision manifests in the implementation.\n    70→\n    71→**When they are never updated.** A stale ADR is worse than no ADR. An ADR with status \"Accepted\" for a decision that was reversed six months ago actively misleads anyone who reads it. If the team does not have the discipline to update ADR statuses when decisions change — marking old ADRs as \"Superseded by ADR-NNN\" and writing new ADRs for the replacement decisions — the ADR log becomes a historical fiction that erodes trust in all architectural documentation. Teams that cannot maintain ADR status should not start an ADR practice until they solve the maintenance problem, typically by integrating ADR review into their regular retrospective or architectural review cadence.\n    72→\n    73→**Solo projects with no expected team growth.** A single developer working on a personal project or a prototype with no expectation of collaboration does not need ADRs. The developer's own memory and the git history are sufficient. The ROI of ADRs comes from communication across people and across time — if there is only one person and no expectation of a future audience, the overhead of formal decision records exceeds the benefit. The exception is when the solo developer expects to hand the project off or to return to it after a long absence; in those cases, ADRs serve as messages to a future self who will have forgotten the original context.\n    74→\n    75→**When the team is moving too fast for the format.** During a one-week hackathon, a two-week spike, or the first 48 hours of incident response, stopping to write formal ADRs is counterproductive. The decisions being made are either temporary (and will be revisited when the pressure eases) or urgent (and the time spent documenting them is time not spent resolving the incident). The right approach is to capture decisions informally during the crunch — a Slack message, a comment in the PR, a bullet in the incident timeline — and formalize them into ADRs afterward if they turn out to be durable.\n    76→\n    77→**When ADRs substitute for conversation.** If a team uses ADRs as a way to avoid face-to-face (or video-call) discussion — writing a long ADR and posting it for asynchronous approval rather than having a 30-minute conversation with the affected parties — the format is being misused. ADRs document the outcome of a decision process; they do not replace the process itself. The conversation produces alignment and shared understanding; the ADR preserves it.\n    78→\n    79→---\n    80→"}]},"uuid":"69ac2b30-09ad-4972-a769-2f122801efcd","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:36:49.223Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"672c10b9-f783-4fe4-8576-5b3edcc4f125","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

{"parentUuid":"023a5622-f032-4409-ad45-0c6e9b39c796","isSidechain":true,"promptId":"67fcde4a-7483-4526-a2da-9ae9514890c8","agentId":"a722af01c5594aba0","type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01AkaGLtGRBADKbL83kj7v8i","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→# CQRS and Event Sourcing — Architecture Expertise Module\n     2→\n     3→> CQRS separates read and write models. Event Sourcing stores state as a sequence of immutable events. They often appear together but are independent patterns — CQRS is common and useful; Event Sourcing is powerful but niche and complex.\n     4→\n     5→> **Category:** Pattern\n     6→> **Complexity:** Expert\n     7→> **Applies when:** Systems with asymmetric read/write loads, audit requirements, temporal queries, or complex domain logic needing full history\n     8→\n     9→---\n    10→\n    11→## What This Is (and What It Isn't)\n    12→\n    13→### CQRS: Command Query Responsibility Segregation\n    14→\n    15→Gregory Young coined the term CQRS around 2010, building on Bertrand Meyer's Command Query Separation (CQS) principle from 1988. CQS says a method should either change state (command) or return data (query), never both. CQRS elevates this from a method-level principle to an architectural-level one: the system should have **separate models** for writing data and reading data.\n    16→\n    17→The **command side** receives commands (intentions to change state), validates them against business rules, and applies state changes. The **query side** reads data and returns it in whatever shape the consumer needs. These two sides can have entirely different data models, different optimization strategies, and — crucially — can be scaled independently.\n    18→\n    19→**What CQRS does NOT require:**\n    20→\n    21→- **Two databases.** The simplest CQRS implementation uses a single database with separate read and write models in the application layer. The read model might be a set of denormalized views or materialized query objects. The write model might be a rich domain model. They share a database, and that is fine.\n    22→- **Event Sourcing.** CQRS works perfectly with a traditional relational database using CRUD persistence. You can have a normalized write model and denormalized read projections in the same PostgreSQL instance.\n    23→- **Eventual consistency.** If both models share a database, reads are strongly consistent. Eventual consistency only enters when you separate the read database from the write database and synchronize them asynchronously.\n    24→- **Messaging infrastructure.** You can implement CQRS without a message bus. Commands can be method calls. Queries can be method calls. The pattern is about model separation, not infrastructure separation.\n    25→\n    26→### Event Sourcing: State as a Sequence of Facts\n    27→\n    28→Event Sourcing stores the state of a system as an append-only sequence of immutable events, rather than as a mutable current-state record. Instead of storing \"account balance = $500\", you store the series of events that led to that balance: `AccountOpened($0)`, `MoneyDeposited($1000)`, `MoneyWithdrawn($300)`, `MoneyDeposited($200)`, `MoneyWithdrawn($400)`.\n    29→\n    30→The current state is derived by replaying events from the beginning (or from a snapshot). The event log is the single source of truth. The current-state representation is a disposable projection that can be rebuilt at any time.\n    31→\n    32→**What Event Sourcing IS:**\n    33→\n    34→- An **append-only** store of domain events. Events are never updated or deleted.\n    35→- A **complete audit trail** by construction, not as an afterthought.\n    36→- A mechanism for **temporal queries** — you can reconstruct the state of the system at any point in time by replaying events up to that moment.\n    37→- A foundation for **event replay** — you can rebuild projections, fix bugs in projection logic, and create new read models from historical events.\n    38→\n    39→**What Event Sourcing IS NOT:**\n    40→\n    41→- **Not event-driven architecture.** Publishing events between services (via Kafka, RabbitMQ, etc.) is event-driven architecture. Storing state as events is event sourcing. You can have event-driven architecture without event sourcing, and event sourcing without event-driven architecture (though they complement each other).\n    42→- **Not a logging mechanism.** An event log is not an application log. Events are domain-meaningful state transitions, not debug output. `OrderPlaced` is an event. `Database query took 200ms` is a log entry.\n    43→- **Not a message queue.** The event store is a database, not a transport. Events are persisted for state reconstruction, not for delivery to consumers (though consumers can subscribe to them).\n    44→\n    45→### They Are Separate Patterns\n    46→\n    47→This is the most critical misconception to address. Greg Young himself has stated that while he introduced CQRS and Event Sourcing together, they are independent patterns. His later reflection: \"You need to look at CQRS not as being the main thing. CQRS was a product of its time and meant to be a stepping stone towards the ideas of Event Sourcing.\"\n    48→\n    49→However, there is an asymmetry: **you can use CQRS without Event Sourcing** (and this is the more common case), but **if you use Event Sourcing, you almost always need CQRS**. The reason is pragmatic: querying an event store directly for read operations is extremely inefficient. You would need to replay events for every query. Projections (read models) solve this, and projections are the query side of CQRS.\n    50→\n    51→**The four combinations:**\n    52→\n    53→| | Without CQRS | With CQRS |\n    54→|---|---|---|\n    55→| **Without ES** | Traditional CRUD (most apps) | CQRS with CRUD persistence (common, useful) |\n    56→| **With ES** | Technically possible but impractical | ES with CQRS (the canonical ES setup) |\n    57→\n    58→### Common Misconceptions\n    59→\n    60→**\"CQRS means two databases.\"** No. CQRS is about separate models, not separate storage. A single PostgreSQL instance with a normalized write schema and denormalized read views is CQRS. Read replicas alone are NOT CQRS — they are infrastructure scaling. CQRS requires different data models for reads and writes, not just copies of the same model.\n    61→\n    62→**\"Event Sourcing gives you free debugging.\"** Partially true. You can replay events to reproduce state, but most production bugs in event-sourced systems come from bad events caused by human error or flawed business logic, not from application crashes. Replaying bad events reproduces the bad state faithfully — that is not debugging, that is archaeology.\n    63→\n    64→**\"Event Sourcing means you never lose data.\"** You never lose the history of what happened. But if your projection logic has a bug, your read model can diverge from reality silently for months. The events are correct; the interpretation of them is where data loss happens.\n    65→\n    66→**\"We need Event Sourcing for our audit trail.\"** Maybe. But an audit table (a separate append-only log of changes) achieves 80% of the audit benefit at 10% of the complexity. Event Sourcing is warranted when you need the audit trail AND temporal queries AND event replay AND the ability to rebuild state from scratch. If you just need \"who changed what, when\" for compliance, a CDC-based audit log or database triggers may suffice.\n    67→\n    68→---\n    69→\n    70→## When to Use It\n    71→\n    72→### When to Use CQRS (Without Event Sourcing)\n    73→\n    74→**Asymmetric read/write loads.** When reads outnumber writes by 5:1 or more, and the read access patterns differ from the write model's shape. An e-commerce catalog where writes update product details (normalized) but reads need denormalized product cards with pricing, images, reviews, and availability. A social media feed where writes are individual posts but reads need aggregated, ranked, filtered timelines.\n    75→\n    76→**Complex domain logic on the write side.** When the write model involves rich validation, business rules, and invariant enforcement that would be polluted by read-side concerns. A financial trading platform where order placement involves margin checks, position limits, and risk calculations — but the read side just needs a dashboard of open positions. Keeping these models separate prevents the query optimization from contaminating the command validation.\n    77→\n    78→**Multiple read representations of the same data.** A single write model needs to produce different read models for different consumers: an API response, an analytics dashboard, a search index, a report. Rather than one monolithic model that serves all purposes poorly, CQRS lets each read model be optimized for its consumer.\n    79→\n    80→**Teams with separate read/write ownership.** When the write side is owned by the domain team and the read side is owned by the platform or analytics team, CQRS provides a natural boundary for team ownership and independent deployment."}]},"uuid":"427b6504-c43b-477a-8d3c-b236d054da8a","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:37:37.630Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"023a5622-f032-4409-ad45-0c6e9b39c796","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

business-critical paths:\n   149→\n   150→```python\n   151→from opentelemetry import trace\n   152→\n   153→tracer = trace.get_tracer(\"payment-service\")\n   154→\n   155→def process_payment(order):\n   156→    with tracer.start_as_current_span(\"process_payment\") as span:\n   157→        span.set_attribute(\"order.amount\", order.amount)\n   158→        span.set_attribute(\"order.currency\", order.currency)\n   159→\n   160→        with tracer.start_as_current_span(\"validate_card\"):\n   161→            validate(order.card)  # creates child span\n   162→\n   163→        with tracer.start_as_current_span(\"charge_provider\"):\n   164→            result = charge(order)  # creates child span\n   165→            span.set_attribute(\"payment.provider_latency_ms\", result.latency)\n   166→\n   167→        return result\n   168→```\n   169→\n   170→**Guidelines for custom spans:**\n   171→\n   172→- Instrument operations taking >1ms that cross boundaries (network, disk, queue).\n   173→- Add business attributes (order value, customer tier) for filtering.\n   174→- Avoid spans inside tight loops (creating a span costs ~1 microsecond, but at 1M iterations\n   175→  that adds 1 second of pure overhead).\n   176→- Use span events for lightweight annotations instead of child spans.\n   177→\n   178→### The OTel Collector\n   179→\n   180→The Collector sits between instrumented applications and backends, providing:\n   181→\n   182→- **Receivers**: Accept data via OTLP, Jaeger, Zipkin, Prometheus, and 80+ formats.\n   183→- **Processors**: Batch, filter, sample, transform, and enrich telemetry.\n   184→- **Exporters**: Send to any backend (Jaeger, Tempo, Prometheus, Datadog, etc.).\n   185→\n   186→**Performance characteristics of the Collector:**\n   187→\n   188→- Batching processor: groups spans into batches of 8192 (default), reducing export overhead\n   189→  by 10-50x compared to per-span export.\n   190→- Memory limiter processor: prevents OOM by dropping data when memory exceeds threshold\n   191→  (recommended: set to 80% of available memory).\n   192→- Typical resource usage: 0.5-2 CPU cores and 512 MB-2 GB RAM for 50,000 spans/second\n   193→  throughput (OTel Collector benchmarks).\n   194→\n   195→### Reducing OTel Overhead\n   196→\n   197→Strategies that reduce CPU overhead by 50-70% (OneUptime, 2026):\n   198→\n   199→1. **Selective instrumentation**: Disable instrumentations you don't need\n   200→   (e.g., `OTEL_INSTRUMENTATION_HTTP_ENABLED=false`).\n   201→2. **Increase batch size**: Larger batches amortize export cost; 8192+ spans per batch.\n   202→3. **Use sampling**: Even 10% sampling reduces CPU overhead by ~80% (Umea research).\n   203→4. **Async exporters**: Never block the application thread on telemetry export.\n   204→5. **Filter at the Collector**: Drop low-value spans before export to reduce backend load.\n   205→\n   206→---\n   207→\n   208→## Distributed Tracing Systems\n   209→\n   210→### Jaeger\n   211→\n   212→Originally developed by Uber, now a CNCF graduated project.\n   213→\n   214→- **Jaeger 2.0** (November 2024): Rebuilt on the OpenTelemetry Collector framework.\n   215→  Single binary reduced image size from 40 MB to 30 MB. Native OTLP support eliminates\n   216→  translation overhead. '' : notice,\n   433→    };\n   434→  }\n   435→\n   436→  // Record the event and update loop count in status.json\n   437→  const status = readStatus(runPaths);\n   438→  const loopCount = parsePositiveInteger(options.loopCount, '--loop-count');\n   439→  const loopPhase = options.phase;\n   440→  const loopKey = options.taskId ? `${loopPhase}:${options.taskId}` : loopPhase;\n   441→\n   442→  status.phase_loop_counts = {\n   443→    ...(status.phase_loop_counts ?? {}),\n   444→    [loopKey]: loopCount,\n   445→  };\n   446→\n   447→  const event = createBaseEvent('loop_iteration', {\n   448→    run_id: options.run,\n   449→    phase: loopPhase,\n   450→    status: status.status,\n   451→    loop_count: loopCount,\n   452→    loop_key: loopKey,\n   453→  });\n   454→\n   455→  if (options.taskId) {\n   456→    event.task_id = options.taskId;\n   457→  }\n   458→\n   459→  status.updated_at = event.created_at;\n   460→  status.last_event = 'loop_iteration';\n   461→\n   462→  appendEvent(runPaths, event);\n   463→  writeStatus(runPaths, status);\n   464→\n   465→  // Read run-config for loop_cap\n   466→  const runConfig = readRunConfig(runPaths);\n   467→  const loopCap = getPhaseLoopCap(runConfig, loopPhase);\n   468→\n   469→  // Evaluate the guard using loopKey (task-scoped or phase-scoped).\n   470→  // Cap is per-phase but counts are per-task — each task gets its own\n   471→  // budget up to the phase cap. This is intentional: task-scoped tracking\n   472→  // prevents parallel tasks from sharing a single counter.\n   473→  const guardResult = evaluateLoopCapGuard({\n   474→    run_id: options.run,\n   475→    phase: loopKey,\n   476→    state_root: stateRoot,\n   477→    loop_cap: loopCap,\n   478→  });\n   479→\n   480→  if (!guardResult.allowed) {\n   481→    return {\n   482→      exitCode: 43,\n   483→      stderr: `${guardResult.reason}\\n`,\n   484→      stdout: options.json ? `${JSON.stringify(guardResult, null, 2)}\\n` : '',\n   485→    };\n   486→  }\n   487→\n   488→  return formatResult({\n   489→    run_id: options.run,\n   490→    phase: loopPhase,\n   491→    loop_key: loopKey,\n   492→    loop_count: loopCount,\n   493→    loop_cap: loopCap,\n   494→    allowed: true,\n   495→  }, { json: options.json });\n   496→}\n   497→\n   498→export function runCaptureCommand(parsed, context = {}) {\n   499→  try {\n   500→    switch (parsed.subcommand) {\n   501→      case 'init':\n   502→        return handleInit(parsed, context);\n   503→      case 'event':\n   504→        return handleEvent(parsed, context);\n   505→      case 'route':\n   506→        return handleRoute(parsed, context);\n   507→      case 'output':\n   508→        return handleOutput(parsed, context);\n   509→      case 'summary':\n   510→        return handleSummary(parsed, context);\n   511→      case 'usage':\n   512→        return handleUsage(parsed, context);\n   513→      case 'loop-check':\n   514→        return handleLoopCheck(parsed, context);\n   515→      case 'decision':\n   516→        return handleDecision(parsed, context);\n   517→      default:\n   518→        return {\n   519→          exitCode: 1,\n   520→          stderr: 'Usage: wazir capture <init|event|route|output|summary|usage|loop-check|decision> ...\\n',\n   521→        };\n   522→    }\n   523→  } catch (error) {\n   524→    return {\n   525→      exitCode: 1,\n   526→      stderr: `${error.message}\\n`,\n   527→    };\n   528→  }\n   529→}\n   530→"}]},"uuid":"749fd0b6-9b0c-4ff6-9965-b07bba20bab9","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:38:00.075Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1931ce22-27e7-4186-8939-ebf2e54e5017","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

Each span has a unique Span ID and a Parent Span ID pointing to the span that created it.\n\n## Trace ID and Span ID Format\n\nA valid trace identifier is a 16-byte array with at least one non-zero byte. A valid span identifier is an 8-byte array with at least one non-zero byte.\n\n## Span Attributes Schema\n\nSpan attributes are key-value pairs that provide important context for a span, such as HTTP methods, user IDs, or transaction identifiers. Values must be a non-null string, boolean, floating point value, integer, or an array of these values.\n\n## Span Events Structure\n\nAn Event is structurally defined by the following properties: Name of the event. A timestamp for the event. Either the time at which the event was added or a custom timestamp provided by the user. Zero or more Attributes further describing the event.\n\nEvents are small, time-stamped entries that record important moments inside a Span. OpenTelemetry span events, security audit trails, and structured events in 2026:\n\n## OpenTelemetry Span Events for Security Audit Trails\n\nOpenTelemetry span events can be used to build a structured security audit trail that satisfies compliance requirements like SOC 2 and HIPAA. If you are already using OpenTelemetry for observability, you can leverage span events to build your audit trail directly within your existing telemetry pipeline.\n\n## Key Benefits of This Approach\n\nSpan events are timestamped records attached to a specific span, and they inherit the span's trace context, which means every audit entry is automatically correlated with the request that triggered it. OpenTelemetry span events provide a natural way to implement audit trails that are automatically correlated with your application traces, allowing you to get structured, queryable audit data that flows through your existing telemetry pipeline instead of maintaining a separate audit logging system.\n\n## SOC 2 Compliance Implementation\n\nBuilding a SOC 2 audit trail on top of OpenTelemetry is a genuinely good architecture choice, as you get structured data, correlation across services via trace IDs, and a pipeline you can version-control and test. Audit data should not be mixed with regular observability telemetry; the OpenTelemetry Collector should route audit-relevant spans to a dedicated, tamper-evident store.\n\n## Important Note on Recent Changes\n\nIt's worth noting that OpenTelemetry is deprecating the Span Event API as of March 17, 2026, to remove confusion and duplication caused by having two overlapping ways to emit events, with new code recommended to write events as logs that are correlated with the current span.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"5ef7f215-3741-4f13-9b5b-31a81066c307","timestamp":"2026-03-20T13:40:09.372Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"5c2c0d4a-af91-42a3-ba39-8d96b3bffe59","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/Users/mohamedabdallah/Work/Wazir","sessionId":"0c5a9bfa-6816-42b1-a97d-37ae7dad26c5","version":"2.1.80","gitBranch":"feat/remaining-6-fixes","slug":"mighty-wondering-giraffe"}

{"parentUuid":"3d38fe7a-bfaa-47d5-92a5-ec93fb271e76","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a722af01c5594aba0","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01H7eBPbmJcv9MdF93nH5z4M","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"\n\nI now have comprehensive research material. Let me synthesize everything into the design output.\n\n---\n\n# Decision Audit Trail Design for Wazir\n\n## 1. Research Findings\n\n### 1A. OpenTelemetry Traces -- How Spans Track Decisions Through a Pipeline\n\nOpenTelemetry models work as directed acyclic graphs (DAGs) of **spans**. Each span has a `trace_id` (16-byte, shared across the entire request), a `span_id` (8-byte, unique to this unit of work), and a `parent_span_id` (linking child to parent). This creates a tree structure where any node can be traced back to the root.\n\n**Key design patterns relevant to Wazir:**\n\n- **Context propagation.** Every child span automatically inherits the trace context of its parent. In a pipeline, this means a decision made in the \"clarify\" phase can be correlated with an outcome in the \"execute\" phase without explicit manual linking -- the shared `trace_id` is the correlation key.\n- **Span attributes as decision metadata.** OTel spans carry key-value attributes (string, bool, number, array). For audit purposes, attributes like `decision.type`, `decision.confidence`, `decision.alternatives_count` can be attached at creation time, which is when sampling decisions are made.\n- **Span events as lightweight annotations.** Events are timestamped records attached to a span with a name and optional attributes. They are ideal for recording \"something happened inside this unit of work\" without creating a full child span. OneUptime demonstrated using span events for SOC 2 audit trails where each event carries structured audit data that inherits the span's trace context automatically.\n- **Important deprecation note (March 2026).** OpenTelemetry is deprecating the Span Events API in favor of correlated log records. The new recommendation is to emit events as structured logs with a `span_id` correlation field. This aligns better with Wazir's NDJSON approach -- structured log lines are the future, not embedded span events.\n\n**Takeaway for Wazir:** The trace/span model validates Wazir's existing two-file approach (`events.ndjson` for operational events, `decisions.ndjson` for decision records). The key insight is the **correlation ID pattern** -- a shared `run_id` + `phase` + `task_id` triple acts as Wazir's equivalent of `trace_id` + `span_id` + `parent_span_id`.\n\n### 1B. MLflow Experiment Tracking -- How ML Pipelines Track Hyperparameter Decisions\n\nMLflow's tracking model uses a hierarchy of **experiments > runs > child runs** with logged **parameters, metrics, and artifacts**.\n\n**Key design patterns relevant to Wazir:**\n\n- **Parent-child run hierarchy.** A parent run represents the overall experiment; child runs represent individual trials (e.g., hyperparameter combinations). Each child run logs its own parameters and metrics independently, but can be queried/compared through the parent. This maps directly to Wazir's `run > phase > task` hierarchy.\n- **Parameters vs. metrics distinction.** Parameters are inputs (decisions made before execution). Metrics are outputs (observed results). MLflow tracks both and enables correlation queries like \"which parameter values produced the best metric outcome?\" This separation is exactly what Wazir needs: decisions are parameters, outcomes (test results, review findings, drift) are metrics.\n- **Automatic logging.** `mlflow.autolog()` captures parameters and metrics without manual instrumentation. This validates the idea that high-value decisions should be captured automatically by hooks, not by requiring agents to manually call `wazir capture decision`.\n- **Comparison UI.** MLflow's power is in post-hoc comparison: scatter plots of parameter vs. metric, parallel coordinate plots showing which parameter combinations led to which outcomes. The audit trail's value is not in writing it -- it is in reading it.\n\n**Takeaway for Wazir:** Separate **decision inputs** (what was decided and why) from **outcome observations** (what happened as a result). Enable correlation queries: \"decisions where reason contained 'scope reduction' AND outcome was 'review_loop > 2 passes'.\"\n\n### 1C. Git Blame + Commit Messages -- Code History as Decision Trail\n\nGit provides a line-level audit trail where `git blame` maps every line to a commit, and the commit message explains the \"why.\" The combination of `git log`, `git blame`, and `git diff` enables tracing from a current code state back through every decision that shaped it.\n\n**Key design patterns relevant to Wazir:**\n\n- **The commit message is the decision record.** Well-written conventional commits (`fix(scope): reason`) serve as micro-ADRs. The body and footer can contain rationale. This is already Wazir's convention.\n- **Blame chains for causality.** `git log --follow` traces a file through renames. `git blame` on a specific line shows the commit that last touched it. This enables \"why does this line exist?\" queries. Wazir's equivalent: \"why was this task in scope?\" requires tracing back through clarification decisions.\n- **Limitations as a decision trail.** Git captures what changed in code, not what was considered and rejected. It does not record \"we considered approach X but rejected it because Y.\" Commit messages can, but rarely do. This is precisely the gap that `decisions.ndjson` fills.\n\n**Takeaway for Wazir:** Git history is a good complement but insufficient alone. `decisions.ndjson` must capture what git cannot: alternatives considered, confidence levels, and the reasoning behind scope/skip decisions that produce no code change at all.\n\n### 1D. Architecture Decision Records (ADRs)\n\nADRs (Nygard, 2011) use a five-section format: Title, Status, Context, Decision, Consequences. AWS, Google Cloud, Microsoft Azure, and UK GDS all recommend them.\n\n**Key design patterns relevant to Wazir:**\n\n- **One decision per record.** ADRs succeed because they are constrained. Each record documents exactly one decision. This maps to one NDJSON line per decision.\n- **Context is the most important section.** What forces were at play? What constraints existed? Without context, the decision is unintelligible six months later. Wazir's `reason` field must carry context, not just rationale.\n- **Status lifecycle.** Proposed -> Accepted -> Deprecated -> Superseded. Decisions can be reversed, but the original record is never deleted -- it gets a status update pointing to the superseding record. Wazir should support decision revision chains.\n- **Signal-to-noise ratio.** The ADR literature is emphatic: too many trivial ADRs destroy the value of the log. The \"would I explain this to a new team member?\" test determines what deserves a record. Wazir must apply the same filter to avoid logging every tool call.\n- **Structured MADR.** The structured-madr project adds YAML frontmatter for machine-readable metadata, risk assessments, and audit sections to traditional ADRs. This validates adding structured fields beyond free text.\n\n**Takeaway for Wazir:** ADR's signal-to-noise discipline is the most important lesson. Log decisions that would need to be explained to a future run's agent or to a human reviewer. Do not log mechanical operations.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Granularity Guidelines\n\n### What to Log (Signal)\n\n| Decision Category | Example | Why It Matters |\n|---|---|---|\n| **Scope decisions** | \"Split task X into subtasks A, B\" | Defines what work will be done |\n| **Skip decisions** | \"Skipping lint check: not configured\" | Explains gaps in output |\n| **Approach selection** | \"Using SQLite over flat files for state\" | Records rejected alternatives |\n| **Scope reduction** | \"Deferring i18n to follow-up run\" | Explains intentional omissions |\n| **Escalation decisions** | \"Escalating ambiguity to user\" | Records agent judgment limits |\n| **Review verdicts** | \"Review pass 2: loop_back, 3 findings\" | Records quality gate outcomes |\n| **Guard overrides** | \"Loop cap exceeded, requesting extension\" | Records policy boundary events |\n| **Confidence-gated choices** | \"Low confidence on API design, adding research\" | Records epistemic state |\n\n### What NOT to Log (Noise)\n\n| Operation Type | Example | Why Exclude |\n|---|---|---|\n| Routine tool calls | \"Ran `npm test`\" | Already in `events.ndjson` |\n| File reads/writes | \"Read package.json\" | Mechanical, no decision |\n| Phase transitions | \"Entering execute phase\" | Already in `events.ndjson` |\n| Successful validations | \"Manifest valid\" | Expected path, no decision |\n| Formatting choices | \"Used 2-space indent\" | Trivial, reversible |\n\n### The Filter Test\n\nA decision should be logged if it meets ANY of these criteria:\n\n1. **Explainability.** A future agent or human would ask \"why was this done this way?\"\n2. **Reversibility cost.** Reversing this decision would require more than 5 minutes of work.\n3. **Alternative rejection.** An alternative was explicitly considered and rejected.\n4. **Confidence uncertainty.** The agent's confidence in the decision was below 0.8.\n5. **Policy boundary.** The decision involved a guard, gate, or cap.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Enhanced Schema Design\n\n### Current Schema (v1 -- what exists today)\n\n```json\n{\n  \"timestamp\": \"2026-03-20T10:00:00.000Z\",\n  \"phase\": \"clarify\",\n  \"decision\": \"Split into two tasks\",\n  \"reason\": \"Scope too large for single implementation\",\n  \"task_id\": \"task-001\"\n}\n```\n\n### Proposed Schema (v2 -- enhanced for audit trail analysis)\n\n```json\n{\n  \"$schema\": \"https://json-schema.org/draft/2020-12/schema\",\n  \"$id\": \"https://wazir.dev/schemas/decision.schema.json\",\n  \"title\": \"Decision Log Entry v2\",\n  \"type\": \"object\",\n  \"required\": [\"timestamp\", \"phase\", \"category\", \"decision\", \"reason\"],\n  \"properties\": {\n    \"timestamp\":    { \"type\": \"string\", \"format\": \"date-time\" },\n    \"phase\":        { \"type\": \"string\", \"minLength\": 1 },\n    \"workflow\":     { \"type\": \"string\", \"description\": \"Specific workflow within phase (e.g., specify, design)\" },\n    \"task_id\":      { \"type\": \"string\" },\n    \"category\":     {\n      \"type\": \"string\",\n      \"enum\": [\"scope\", \"skip\", \"approach\", \"defer\", \"escalate\", \"review_verdict\", \"guard\", \"override\"],\n      \"description\": \"Decision category for filtering and analysis\"\n    },\n    \"decision\":     { \"type\": \"string\", \"maxLength\": 200 },\n    \"reason\":       { \"type\": \"string\", \"maxLength\": 500 },\n    \"context\":      { \"type\": \"string\", \"maxLength\": 500, \"description\": \"Forces/constraints at play (ADR-style context)\" },\n    \"alternatives\": {\n      \"type\": \"array\",\n      \"items\": {\n        \"type\": \"object\",\n        \"required\": [\"option\", \"rejected_because\"],\n        \"properties\": {\n          \"option\":           { \"type\": \"string\" },\n          \"rejected_because\": { \"type\": \"string\" }\n        }\n      },\n      \"description\": \"Considered alternatives and why they were rejected\"\n    },\n    \"confidence\":   {\n      \"type\": \"number\", \"minimum\": 0, \"maximum\": 1,\n      \"description\": \"Agent confidence in this decision (0.0-1.0)\"\n    },\n    \"supersedes\":   {\n      \"type\": \"string\",\n      \"description\": \"Timestamp of the earlier decision this one replaces (revision chain)\"\n    },\n    \"outcome_ref\":  {\n      \"type\": \"string\",\n      \"description\": \"Reference to the outcome that validated/invalidated this decision (e.g., review finding ID, test result)\"\n    }\n  },\n  \"additionalProperties\": false\n}\n```\n\n### Key Additions Over v1\n\n| Field | Inspired By | Purpose |\n|---|---|---|\n| `category` | ADR status taxonomy | Enables filtering: \"show me all scope decisions\" |\n| `workflow` | OTel parent_span_id | Enables drill-down: \"decisions in the specify workflow\" |\n| `context` | ADR Context section | Captures forces at play, not just rationale |\n| `alternatives` | ADR \"options considered\" | Records what was rejected, the most valuable audit data |\n| `confidence` | MLflow metrics | Enables learning: low-confidence decisions that succeeded/failed |\n| `supersedes` | ADR \"Superseded by\" | Enables revision chains without mutating records |\n| `outcome_ref` | MLflow parameter-metric correlation | Enables decision-to-outcome tracing |\n\n### Backward Compatibility\n\nv1 records (missing `category`, `confidence`, etc.) remain valid NDJSON lines. Analysis tools treat missing fields as absent, not as errors. The `readDecisions()` function already tolerates any shape via JSON.parse.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Decision-to-Outcome Correlation\n\nThis is the hardest problem and the highest-value capability.\n\n### Correlation Model\n\n```\nDecision (input)              Outcome (observation)\n─────────────────             ────────────────────\ndecision.ndjson line    ──→   phase-report.json decisions[]\n  timestamp                     attempted_actions[].outcome\n  category: \"approach\"          quality_metrics.test_fail_count\n  task_id: \"task-003\"           drift_analysis.delta\n  confidence: 0.6               risk_flags[].severity\n```\n\n### Correlation Strategies\n\n**Strategy 1: Task-ID join.** When a decision has a `task_id`, join it with the task's review findings, test results, and drift analysis from the phase report. This is the simplest and most reliable correlation.\n\n```\ndecisions WHERE task_id = \"task-003\"\n  JOIN phase_report.attempted_actions WHERE description CONTAINS \"task-003\"\n```\n\n**Strategy 2: Temporal window.** For decisions without a `task_id` (e.g., phase-level scope decisions), correlate with outcomes in a time window. A scope decision made at T correlates with outcomes observed between T and T+phase_duration.\n\n**Strategy 3: Outcome back-reference.** The `outcome_ref` field in v2 allows explicit linking. When a review finding references a decision (\"the approach chosen in task-003 caused coupling issue\"), the reviewer can set `outcome_ref` on the original decision.\n\n**Strategy 4: Category-outcome heuristics.** Certain decision categories have predictable outcome signals:\n\n| Decision Category | Expected Outcome Signal | Bad Signal |\n|---|---|---|\n| `scope` | No drift (drift_analysis.delta = 0) | High drift |\n| `skip` | No review findings about the skipped thing | Review finding: \"missing X\" |\n| `approach` | Tests pass for related task | Test failures in related task |\n| `defer` | No blocking issues in current run | Blocker traced to deferred item |\n| `review_verdict: loop_back` | Next pass has fewer findings | Findings increase or stall |\n\n---\n\n## 5. Using the Audit Trail for Learning\n\nThe `learn` workflow already reads `decisions.ndjson`. The v2 schema makes learning extraction significantly more powerful.\n\n### Learning Extraction Patterns\n\n**Pattern 1: Low-confidence decisions that succeeded.**\n```\ndecisions WHERE confidence < 0.5 AND outcome = success\n→ Proposed learning: \"Approach X works even under constraint Y\"\n```\n\n**Pattern 2: High-confidence decisions that failed.**\n```\ndecisions WHERE confidence > 0.8 AND outcome = failure\n→ Proposed learning: \"Assumption Z was wrong; consider A instead\"\n```\n\n**Pattern 3: Recurring skip decisions.**\n```\ndecisions WHERE category = \"skip\" GROUP BY decision\n  HAVING count(*) > 2 across runs\n→ Proposed learning: \"Feature X is consistently skipped; either enable it or remove it from the pipeline\"\n```\n\n**Pattern 4: Revision chains.**\n```\ndecisions WHERE supersedes IS NOT NULL\n→ The original decision was wrong. What changed? The delta between the original reason\n  and the superseding reason is the learning.\n```\n\n**Pattern 5: Approach decisions with alternatives.**\n```\ndecisions WHERE category = \"approach\" AND alternatives.length > 1\n  AND outcome = failure\n→ Proposed learning: \"Rejected alternative Y might have been better because Z\"\n```\n\n### Integration with Existing Learning Schema\n\nThe `proposed-learning.schema.json` already has `sources` (array of strings) and `confidence` (0-1). Decision-derived learnings would set:\n- `sources`: `[\"decisions.ndjson:L42\", \"phase-report:execute\"]` -- pointing to the specific decision line and the outcome report\n- `confidence`: derived from the strength of the correlation (exact task-ID join = high, temporal window = medium)\n- `scope`: derived from the decision category (approach decisions have project-wide scope; task-level decisions have file-level scope)\n\n---\n\n## 6. Visualization Approaches\n\n### 6A. Timeline View (Primary)\n\nThe most natural visualization for NDJSON data. Each decision is a point on a timeline, color-coded by category, with vertical position indicating phase.\n\n```\nclarify   ●──────●────────────────────────────────────────\nspecify        ●───●──●──────────────────────────────────\ndesign              ●────●───────────────────────────────\nexecute                   ●──●──●──●──●──●──●──●────────\nreview                                         ●──●──●──\nlearn                                                  ●─\n\nLegend: ● scope  ● skip  ● approach  ● defer  ● escalate  ● review\n```\n\n**Implementation:** NDJSON lines sorted by timestamp, grouped by phase. Terminal rendering with ASCII or an HTML report using a simple timeline library. No external services needed (Wazir's file-based constraint).\n\n### 6B. Decision Tree View (For Approach Decisions)\n\nWhen a decision has `alternatives`, render it as a tree showing the chosen path and rejected branches:\n\n```\nTask-003: State storage\n├── [CHOSEN] SQLite (reason: query support, confidence: 0.7)\n│   └── outcome: 662/663 tests pass ✓\n├── [REJECTED] Flat JSON files (rejected: no query capability)\n└── [REJECTED] PostgreSQL (rejected: overhead for CLI tool)\n```\n\n### 6C. Diff View (For Revision Chains)\n\nWhen `supersedes` links exist, show the before/after:\n\n```\nORIGINAL (T1): \"Use flat files for state\" (confidence: 0.5)\n  reason: \"Simplest possible approach\"\nREVISED  (T2): \"Use SQLite for state\" (confidence: 0.8)  [supersedes T1]\n  reason: \"Need query support for phase reports\"\n  context: \"Flat files required full scan for every query\"\n```\n\n### 6D. Correlation Matrix (For Post-Run Analysis)\n\nA summary table showing decisions vs. outcomes:\n\n```\nDecision              Category   Confidence  Outcome      Signal\n─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────\nSplit into 2 tasks   scope      0.9         ✓ no drift   good\nSkip lint check      skip       0.6         ✗ 3 errors   bad  ←learn\nUse SQLite           approach   0.7         ✓ tests pass good\nDefer i18n           defer      0.8         ✓ no blocker good\n```\n\n### Implementation Priority\n\n1. **Timeline view** -- lowest effort, highest immediate value, works in terminal\n2. **Correlation matrix** -- medium effort, highest learning value, works in terminal\n3. **Decision tree** -- medium effort, useful for approach decisions, HTML report\n4. **Diff view** -- low effort, useful for revision chains, works in terminal\n\n---\n\n## 7. Summary of Recommendations\n\n**Schema:** Upgrade from v1 to v2 by adding `category`, `workflow`, `context`, `alternatives`, `confidence`, `supersedes`, and `outcome_ref`. Maintain backward compatibility -- v1 lines remain valid.\n\n**Granularity:** Apply the five-criteria filter test. Log scope, skip, approach, defer, escalate, review verdict, and guard decisions. Do not log routine tool calls, file operations, or successful validations. Target 5-20 decisions per run, not 50-200.\n\n**Correlation:** Use task-ID joins as the primary correlation strategy. Add temporal window correlation for phase-level decisions. Support explicit back-references via `outcome_ref` for reviewer-identified correlations.\n\n**Learning:** Feed low-confidence-but-successful and high-confidence-but-failed decisions into the learn workflow as high-priority proposed learnings. Track recurring skip decisions across runs as process improvement signals.\n\n**Visualization:** Start with terminal-rendered timeline and correlation matrix views. These require no external dependencies and align with Wazir's file-based, host-native design constraint.\n\n**Automatic capture:** Hook-based automatic capture for guard decisions, review verdicts, and escalations. Agent-prompted capture for scope, approach, and defer decisions (these require reasoning that only the agent has).\n\nSources:\n- [OpenTelemetry Traces Concepts](https://opentelemetry.io/docs/concepts/signals/traces/)\n- [OpenTelemetry Tracing API Specification](https://opentelemetry.io/docs/specs/otel/trace/api/)\n- [OpenTelemetry Span Events for
